"That lesson is precisely why Clean Command must be temporary, audited, and purpose-bound."

He sent the proposal.

It was beautifully built.

Tali hated that too.

Clean Command would last thirty-six hours, automatically dissolving after docking stabilization or external safe-hold transfer. It would produce audit logs. It would include a Moonwake liaison, a survivor observer, an Orison context officer, and a Glassward consent consultant. It would prohibit permanent custody determinations. It would authorize one command channel for docking, health triage, warning extraction, and route stabilization.

There.

Warning extraction.

Nema saw it at the same moment.

"Extraction is not review."

Valech said, "Emergency modeling cannot wait for full cultural context."

Oru-Kel's translation came sharp:

"Then your model waits outside truth."

Valech did not flinch.

"With respect, carried warning patterns are valuable because they contain timing information. We can integrate timing without adopting every custodial practice attached to it."

Oru-Kel answered, "You want the pulse without the body that carries it."

"I want the route not to kill people while we debate embodiment."

That sentence would play well outside.

Tali knew it immediately. Every frightened station, every parent watching a countdown, every official who wanted the clean relief of a single command voice would hear Valech as the adult in the room.

Maybe he was.

That was the danger.

Adults in rooms had abandoned Cinder. Adults had built REDLINE. Adults had called Tavi-claims receipts. Adults had treated Orison refusal as obstruction. Adults had written consent where practical into children's songs.

Mase asked, "What happens if Moonwake refuses Clean Command?"

Valech said, "Emergency Reception will reserve safety override."

"Remote handshake?"

"If necessary."

The room changed.

Not surprise.

Confirmation.

Ira said, "You are threatening the thing this table was built to prevent."

"I am naming the consequence of failing to stabilize an approach corridor."

Vela said, "With a launch-era trust phrase you still have not sourced."

Valech looked at her.

"The trust phrase was drawn from lawful reception archives."

Mars channel opened.

Sava Djen said, "Mars did not authorize use."

Earth channel opened.

Minister Reiss said, "Earth did not authorize use."

Lael Shun said, "Compact observer requests provenance."

Valech said, "Provenance can be reviewed under Clean Command."

Tali almost admired the efficiency. Every objection became an argument for the authority he wanted.

She wrote:

Clean Command consumes its own objections.

Then she sent the line privately to Ira.

Ira read it, looked up, and said aloud, "The proposal is structurally hungry. It makes every unresolved question a reason to feed it more authority."

Valech turned to her.

"Counsel, unresolved questions do not stop physics."

"No. But they identify where power will hide once physics is invoked."

"People may die while you identify hiding places."

"People have died because men like you called hiding places command."

Silence.

Tali did not know Ira Senn well enough to know whether the sentence was too much. The Ash Archive room seemed to know. Vela did not move. Lael did not correct. Oru-Kel's band held steady.

Valech accepted the blow without visible anger.

"I served during the Fracture Wars."

Ira said, "That is not a credential here."

"It is experience."

"So is being abandoned."

Mase cut in before the exchange became the event.

"Moonwake requests technical demonstration."

Valech's attention returned to him.

"Of?"

"Your unified command model. Show what it saves, what it delays, what it overrides, and who loses appeal during execution."

"We cannot expose sensitive route models in open custody table."

Vela said, "Then you cannot use them to take command in open custody table."

Valech's jaw tightened slightly.

First visible irritation.

Good.

Tali said, "Moonwake will not accept Clean Command without model disclosure sufficient for ship-law review, descendant quorum, carried-warning context, consent review, Ash Archive objection, and Compact constraint."

"That is impossible in sixteen hours."

Nema said, "Then your proposal is impossible under consent."

"Consent cannot be the only operating principle in emergency."

Sera Venn-3's protected channel opened unexpectedly.

Only text appeared:

Emergency is where they always ask us to become one thing.

No one spoke for several seconds.

Tali had read the Cinder materials. Not all. Enough. Sera's line crossed the table like a hand placed over an old wound before anyone could press it.

Valech said, carefully, "No party is being asked to become one thing."

Sera's channel answered:

Your chart says liaison.

Tali looked back at the proposal.

Moonwake liaison. Orison context. Glassward consultant. Survivor observer.

One command.

Many reductions.

Mase said, "Moonwake rejects Clean Command as written."

Valech said, "Noted."

"Moonwake offers constrained coordination."

"Insufficient."

"You have not seen it."

"I know the time."

"You know the clock," Mase said. "You do not yet know the ship."

Tali began drafting before he ordered her.

Constrained Coordination Counterproposal:

Separate command lanes for docking mechanics, life-support protection, record custody, warning context, medical aid, and exit petitions.

Each lane has named authority, veto triggers, audit log, emergency escalation, and time limit.

No lane may consume another lane by declaring urgency without table review.

Moonwake retains hull command.

External technical teams may advise and submit evidence.

Warning material remains contextual.

Children, witnesses, records, and biological libraries remain outside operational command.

Valech read as she wrote.

"That will fail."

Tali said, "Then demonstrate where."

"The transitions."

"Good. Mark them."

He blinked.

"What?"

"If the transitions fail, mark them. We can reinforce transitions without giving you the whole structure."

For the first time, Valech looked not angry, but interested despite himself.

That was more frightening.

He was not a fool. A fool would have been easier to defeat.

"Send your counterproposal," he said. "I will mark failure points."

Mase approved.

The counterproposal went out.

The approach clock reached fifteen hours thirty-eight minutes.

The Clean Command Proposal remained on the table, objected to, preserved, not dead.

Outside, public channels had already named Valech's plan the adult option.

Moonwake's public summary answered with one sentence:

Adult is not the same as lawful.

Tali did not know who wrote it.

She hoped it was Iven.


Chapter 9: Oru-Kel Refuses Extraction

Oru-Kel refused in a language the table had to feel before it could translate.

The Orison channel did not open with text, image, or diplomatic tone. It opened through Moonwake's old hull as a pressure rhythm along the legal gallery rail: three slow pulses, a pause long enough to make humans want to fill it, then a counterpulse that arrived through the floor rather than the hand.

Tali felt it in her wrist bones.

She stopped writing.

Mase stopped speaking.

Even Valech, who had been marking transition failures in the constrained coordination draft with the calm appetite of a surgeon, looked up.

The table translation lagged by seven seconds.

Oru-Kel-Under-Quiet speaks refusal. Warning cannot be extracted without changing what it warns.

Valech's marker paused.

"No one proposes changing it."

The rail pulsed again.

This time the translation came sharper:

You propose taking timing from relation and calling the remainder culture.

Ren Okoye joined the table from the Shoals Compact channel, hair damp, eyes red with speed-wake. Third-Voice-of-Reef appeared as three parallel chemical bands beside him, translated by an interface that looked too simple for what it carried.

Ren said, "Oru-Kel requests human-language support."

Valech nodded. "Welcome."

Ren did not return the courtesy. "Do not welcome us into theft."

Tali saw Ira Senn's face change in the Ash Archive frame.

There were sentences that entered a room already carrying precedent.

Valech said, "The Clean Command proposal is tabled. We are discussing technical integration."

Oru-Kel's rhythm struck the rail.

Technical is not innocent.

Ren translated before the system did.

"The warning is carried as relation. Its timing pattern was preserved through bodies, refusals, pauses, and obligations. If you strip the pulses and discard the obligations, the model you build will be false."

"False how?" Valech asked.

Good question.

Tali hated that too.

Oru-Kel answered through Ren and the rail together.

"You will know when to move mass. You will not know when not to move mass."

Silence.

There it was.

The missing half of every emergency model Moonwake had seen so far. Clean Command could move. Routeholders could move. Earth could process. Mars could produce old reasons. Even Ash Archive procedures moved records from danger to custody. But the next-gate warning, if Oru-Kel was right, contained refusal timing: the moments when action must stop so survival would not become selection.

Mase said, "Give an example."

Third-Voice's chemical bands shifted. Ren watched them with the concentration of someone listening through skin.

"There is a pulse in the Moonwake index that human instruments mark as delay noise. Orison carry law marks it as grief hold."

Valech said, "Grief hold is not a navigation category."

Ren said, "It should be."

"Explain operationally."

Oru-Kel's answer came as a long low pressure through the deck, and for a moment Tali felt the legal gallery become less like a room and more like a throat.

Translation:

When a carried warning names a loss that cannot be corrected, motion pauses long enough to prevent the next decision from treating that loss as available margin.

Ira whispered, "Cinder."

Not into the channel, but the table caught it anyway.

Valech heard.

"You are importing tribunal grief into route control."

Ira said, "No. We are identifying how route control becomes atrocity when grief is treated as inefficiency."

Valech turned back to Ren.

"Can Orison provide the warning pattern with operational annotations?"

Ren said, "Under context law, yes."

"Can those annotations be converted into decision thresholds?"

"Some."

"Can they be executed in under fifteen hours?"

Ren looked to Third-Voice. Third-Voice's bands rippled disagreement with itself.

"Not if you require every authority to understand them first."

Valech spread one hand. "That is my point."

Oru-Kel's rail pulse came before Ren could speak.

No. Your point is that understanding takes too long, so obedience should replace it.

Tali wrote the sentence exactly.

Mase saw her.

"Public?"

"Not yet."

"Why?"

"Because if public fear hears that, it becomes theater. The table needs it as law first."

He accepted that.

The custody table shifted from argument to construction. Ira asked for categories. Vela asked for access limits. Lael asked who could bind the Compact to a carried-warning rule. Nema asked whether Orison context could prevent memory extraction by analogy. Mase asked what Moonwake had to carry, not merely receive.

Valech asked where the model would fail.

That was why he remained dangerous. He learned from opposition without surrendering his appetite.

Oru-Kel named four protections:

First: no pulse without carry note.

Second: no carry note without refusal history.

Third: no refusal history without living custodian.

Fourth: no command use without pause authority.

Valech objected to the fourth.

"Pause authority vested outside operational command can paralyze response."

Oru-Kel answered:

Operational command without pause authority can weaponize response.

Lael said, "We have precedent for that."

No one needed the list.

Tali thought of Cinder's abandoned corridors, the Shoals refusal votes, the mirror-door trafficking ledgers, Mars children counted as substrate, Kestrel schedules rewritten around those allowed to matter. The Meridian had been teaching this sentence for centuries, if anyone had been listening closely enough.

Valech said, "Who holds pause authority?"

Ren answered, "Not one holder."

"Then no one can use it quickly."

Third-Voice's bands flashed; Ren smiled without humor.

"You are still imagining pause as command. It is not. It is a triggered condition. If protected categories are about to be converted into margin, the lane stops until the table reviews."

Tali looked at her constrained coordination draft.

No lane may consume another lane by declaring urgency without table review.

The Orison protection fit.

Not easily.

But it fit.

She inserted a new lane:

Warning context and pause authority: Orison carried-warning custodian with table triggers; no extraction of timing pattern without carry note, refusal history, living custodian, and protected pause conditions.

Valech read it.

"This will slow docking."

Mase said, "How much?"

"Unknown."

"Then mark the transition."

Valech did.

The approach clock reached fourteen hours fifty minutes.

Earth sent a caution that delay increased medical risk.

Mars sent a technical note that pause authority might conflict with origin mission return conditions.

Vela denied routeholder access to the Orison pattern before the request had finished arriving.

Nema asked Oru-Kel whether a carried warning could consent to limited machine modeling.

Oru-Kel answered:

Machine may listen if machine is named listener, not owner.

Nema said, "That distinction should apply to memory traces."

Oru-Kel's pulse softened.

Yes.

Tali noticed the bridge forming: Orison warning, Glassward consent, Ash Archive refusal, Moonwake ship law. Not agreement. Something harder and more useful. A set of ways to prevent useful material from becoming ownerless once powerful people needed it.

Valech noticed too.

He said, "You are building a veto web."

Ira said, "No. A responsibility web."

"Responsibility does not dock ships."

Mase said, "Irresponsibility has docked many."

The table accepted the Orison context lane provisionally.

Then came the harder part.

Oru-Kel would not release the full carried-warning pattern until Moonwake agreed to carry it back.

Tali blinked.

"Back where?"

Ren looked at her through the channel.

"Into your ship law."

"Moonwake did not originate the warning."

"No. Moonwake received late, held late, and now asks others to act on it. Carry is not origin. Carry is obligation."

Tali understood too quickly and not enough.

"You want Moonwake to become custodian?"

Oru-Kel answered through rail and text:

Co-custodian under refusal. Not owner.

Mase said, "What does that require?"

Ren said, "A living carrier. A ship record. A refusal clause. A promise that if the warning saves you, you do not later treat Orison context as decorative."

Tali felt everyone look at her.

Legal carrier.

Of course.

The trap in being good at a thing was that history eventually noticed.

Mase said, "Tali may refuse."

Good.

Necessary.

Not enough to make refusal easy.

Tali looked at the rail under her hand. She thought of her mother's air-cycle rhyme. She thought of Nema saying some songs might be clean. She thought of Iven asking whether they were wrong because the mission had named them wrong. She thought of Cinder not empty, delay does not end duty, adult is not the same as lawful.

She said, "I will carry under conditions."

Oru-Kel waited.

"The carry record must state Moonwake does not own the warning. Orison context remains attached. Any machine model must name the living custodian chain. Any command use must preserve pause authority. Moonwake children will not be taught the warning as obedience."

Ren translated. Third-Voice shifted. Oru-Kel's pulse moved through the rail like distant surf under metal.

Accepted.

The warning did not open.

Not fully.

Only the first layer arrived: a sequence of pulses, pauses, grief holds, refusal marks, and timing intervals. Tali felt them before she saw the translation, and for one vertiginous moment she understood why extraction was a lie. The pattern was not data plus culture. It was decision shaped by having refused the wrong thing once and survived long enough to warn someone else.

She recorded the carry oath.

"Moonwake receives the carried warning under refusal. We carry late. We carry answerable. We do not own what we carry. We do not move so fast that loss becomes margin."

The rail warmed under her palm.

Oru-Kel answered:

Now you may model the first pulse.

Valech exhaled sharply.

"Finally."

Tali looked at him.

"No," she said. "Not finally. Lawfully."

Outside, the approach clock kept falling.

But the table had gained something the clock did not know how to count.

A reason to stop.


Chapter 10: The Descendants Vote No Home Yet

Moonwake did not call the assembly a referendum.

Referendum was a planet word. It suggested ground, districts, weather, walls that did not share pressure, and citizens who could lose an argument and still sleep somewhere that night. Moonwake had votes, but it distrusted any vote that pretended choice was clean when everyone breathed the same repaired air.

The ship called it a descendant hold.

Every kin cluster sent one speaking adult, one silent adult, one youth witness over twelve, and one elder if the elder consented to being carried through the corridors during approach stress. Children under twelve watched from nursery channels unless their parents refused broadcast, which three families did, not because they wanted secrecy but because their youngest had started crying whenever Earth said welcome.

Tali stood at the center of the low ring in Assembly Spine Two and wished the table could see what external authorities meant when they said passenger manifest.

Not passengers.

People wearing ship slippers and patched formal sashes. People with hair braided around air-filter beads, people with old injury braces grown from recycled printer resin, people whose legal names included deck numbers because two births in the same week had required fast distinction during a coolant leak. People holding children who had learned planets as art before planets learned them as evidence.

Mase stood outside the ring.

That mattered.

As captain he could call emergency action, but he could not stand inside a descendant hold unless summoned. Command was custody. Custody had edges. Moonwake remembered that because forgetting would have made the ship easier to run and less worth returning.

The approach clock sat above the assembly hatch.

Fourteen hours eleven minutes.

It was rude enough to be useful.

Tali opened the slate.

"The question before the hold is not whether Moonwake will dock today."

A murmur moved through the ring.

She let it move.

"The question is whether Moonwake consents to classify docking as homecoming before custody terms exist."

The murmur changed.

Good.

Words had to be made heavy in the correct place.

Jeren Pell from Hydroponics Three lifted the green token first. He was not old, but ship stress had put old around his eyes.

"If it is not homecoming, what is it?"

Tali answered from the draft, not from hope.

"A controlled legal contact under unresolved jurisdiction."

Someone laughed bitterly.

Jeren did not.

"Will that let them deny us medical exchange?"

"They may try. Ash Archive and Glassward observers have already marked medical access as non-transfer leverage if withheld."

"Observers do not grow blood foam out of children's lungs."

Mase moved one step and stopped himself before the ring boundary.

Tali felt grateful and angry at once.

"No," she said. "They do not."

Lio Wren, no relation to Nema and tired of saying so, held up the blue token for procedural clarification.

"If we vote no home yet, do we become stateless?"

An elder named Sevi laughed so hard her carrier straps creaked.

"Child, we have been stateless since my grandmother was born."

Lio flushed.

Sevi softened without apologizing.

"But your question is lawful. Ask it."

Tali said, "External authorities will describe us as unreceived. We will describe ourselves as self-custodied pending plural recognition."

"Will they accept that?"

"No."

The answer struck harder because it was plain.

Tali did not soften it.

"Earth has already signaled that receipt creates passenger protection. Mars claims origin mission obligations. Routeholder houses are offering berth under escrow. Compact emergency offices want docking sequence control. Clean Command advocates want the warning modeled before people are settled. None of them currently recognize Moonwake's full descendant law."

Iven Marr stood in the youth witness arc.

He had scrubbed grease off his cheek and missed a streak near his ear.

"Do we recognize it?"

The assembly went very still.

Tali looked at him, then at the adults who had turned toward him as if youth made the question innocent.

It did not.

"That is what this hold decides."

Iven swallowed.

"Because if we say no home yet, we are saying the ship is enough."

"No," Sevi said.

Tali let the elder take it.

Sevi leaned forward in her straps.

"We are saying the ship was enough to keep us alive. That is different from enough forever."

Iven nodded once, fiercely.

"Then I vote no home yet."

"Youth witnesses do not vote," Jeren said.

Iven looked at him.

"I know. I witness loudly."

That broke the room open just enough.

Not laughter. Not quite. A pressure release.

The assembly proceeded through testimony.

A mother from Nursery One spoke for docking because three children needed gravity therapy that Moonwake could no longer simulate without stealing power from algae light. A pressure tech spoke against immediate homecoming because Earth's welcome packet listed "cultural reintegration" before "legal consultation." A teacher asked whether Moonwake schooling would be treated as credential or damage. A medic asked if outside hospitals would record shipborn reproductive adaptations as illness. A burial keeper asked whether nutrient-return dead would count as missing persons, property claims, or family.

Tali wrote every question.

Some she had answers for.

Most she had shapes where answers should be.

Nema joined as an observer and did the most respectful thing possible: she said nothing until asked.

When asked, she said, "Glassward will recognize any vote that preserves personhood before integration."

"Will Glassward house us?" someone demanded.

Nema's face tightened.

"Not without creating another custody problem."

"Then what good is recognition?"

"It can prevent a faster authority from pretending you consented."

That was not enough.

It was also not nothing.

Ira Senn spoke from the Ash Archive channel after the assembly voted to admit her voice. Her image appeared on the wall without a banner, title, or heroic angle. Just a tired advocate in ash-gray light.

"If Moonwake votes no home yet, the Archive can receive protected copies of the custody objection and bind them to public docket. That will make seizure harder to describe as rescue."

"Harder," Jeren said.

"Yes."

"Not impossible."

"Nothing lawful is impossible to violate," Ira said. "Law is what lets violation have a name before power calls it order."

The assembly took that badly.

Then it took it seriously.

Mase requested permission to enter the ring.

The hold made him wait while three procedural stewards counted dissent.

Tali saw his jaw work through the delay.

Good, she thought before she could stop herself.

Not because she wanted him humiliated. Because captains needed to feel the size of a door before entering it.

Permission carried.

Mase stepped inside the ring and removed his command sash.

That sound, cloth sliding from shoulder, did more than his first words.

"I want you docked."

No one moved.

"I want damaged lungs in real hospitals. I want fresh hull plates. I want children to see a sky that is not educational simulation. I want to stop counting filters like prayers. If I could make those things arrive without risk, I would order them and accept censure."

Tali held her breath.

Mase looked around the ring.

"I cannot. Every offer has a hook. Some hooks are kind. Some are hidden. Some may be necessary. But I will not call a hook home because I am tired."

The room received that as if it had been hungry for a captain to name fatigue without making it sovereign.

"Vote no home yet if that is your judgment," he said. "If you vote yes, I will execute it. If you vote no, I will defend it. If you vote fracture, I will remain captain only long enough to keep us from turning that fracture into pressure loss."

Sevi said, "You have been keeping a casualty ledger from us."

The sentence landed like a tool dropped in low gravity: slow, visible, impossible to ignore.

Mase did not look at Tali.

That was how she knew it was true.

"Yes," he said.

The assembly changed again.

Not riot.

Worse for him.

Procedure.

Tokens lifted. Stewards conferred. The casualty question was marked for later censure and excluded from the immediate hold because docking consent could not wait for full captain review. Moonwake's law did not forgive Mase. It did not let anger eat the only question currently capable of keeping the ship whole.

Tali's respect for her people hurt.

The vote took nine minutes.

Green for immediate homecoming under external reception: forty-three.

Amber for docking as controlled contact if custody terms were filed first: one hundred ninety-six.

Red for no docking under present terms: seventy-eight.

Gray for abstain under medical conflict: twenty-one.

Under Moonwake law, amber carried because the question was not mere preference. It met the threshold for conditional refusal: no homecoming language, no passenger transfer, no unrestricted manifest, no warning extraction, no child relocation, no hospital intake without legal escort, no external custody of education archives, and no captain-only waiver.

Tali read the result aloud.

Her voice did not shake until the last line.

"Moonwake does not refuse contact. Moonwake refuses home as a word used to make custody disappear."

The wall channel carried the statement to the table.

Earth responded in twelve seconds:

EARTH WELCOME COMMISSION REGRETS DELAY AND REQUESTS HUMANITARIAN EXCEPTION.

Mars responded in nineteen:

MARS ORIGIN OFFICE REQUESTS CLARIFICATION OF CONDITIONAL DOCKING TERMS.

Valech responded through the emergency table, voice controlled.

"This costs time."

Tali was still standing in the ring when she answered.

"So did leaving."

For once, he had no immediate reply.

Iven, silent now because youth witness did not mean fool, touched the rail beside her.

The assembly began to disperse by pressure section. No triumph. No anthem. No one called it freedom. Freedom was too large and too cheaply used by people with doors.

Mase remained in the ring without his sash.

Sevi looked at him.

"Captain."

"Yes."

"After custody terms, we discuss the ledger."

"Yes."

Tali watched him accept the future arriving for him.

Outside, Moonwake held its course.

Not toward home.

Toward terms.


Chapter 11: A Routeholder Offers Safety

House Ceyr arrived with clean hands, which was how Tali knew to look for the dirt.

The offer did not come through the emergency table. It came through an old commercial courtesy channel embedded in Moonwake's arrival stack, a relic of launch-era convoy law that had survived because no one on the ship had wanted to delete anything labeled safety. The packet waited until the descendant hold result entered public registry, then unfolded into a formal berth guarantee with polished margins, crisis language, and a crest that looked like a gate drawn by someone who had never had to repair one.

HOUSE CEYR OFFERS PROTECTED TRANSIT, MEDICAL BERTH, AND TECHNICAL SHIELDING UNDER NEUTRAL ROUTEHOLDER ESCROW.

Mase read the first line and said, "No."

Tali did not look up from the packet.

"Captain?"

"That was my full analysis."

"It may need to be longer for the record."

He put both hands on the briefing table, command sash restored and somehow heavier after the hold.

"House Ceyr owned pieces of the early Kestrel liability chain. Ceyr families insured half the routes that profited from delay classifications during the war. They are very good at saying neutral while standing on the toll gate."

"Moonwake records list Ceyr as departure sponsor class three," Tali said.

Mase closed his eyes.

"Of course they do."

The packet opened its second layer without permission because courtesy law assumed a polite recipient.

Tali disabled auto-expand and logged the violation.

Small things mattered.

The offer was attractive in the way traps were attractive when they contained what people needed. Ceyr would provide a protected berth outside Earth reception custody, outside Mars origin claim, outside Compact direct docking control. Ceyr medical contractors would receive urgent cases under ship escort. Ceyr tugs would stabilize Moonwake's approach corridor without requiring command transfer. Ceyr would even accept the Orison context lane "as an advisory restraint pending harmonization."

Then came the price.

Record escrow.

Not seizure.

Not surrender.

Escrow.

A beautiful word if you wanted a locked room to sound like trust.

Tali highlighted the relevant clause and sent it to the custody table.

ROUTEHOLDER NEUTRAL ESCROW SHALL RECEIVE DUPLICATE PROTECTED INDEX OF MOONWAKE RETURN RECORDS, WARNING CATALOG, DESCENDANT MANIFEST CLASSES, AND ORIGIN MISSION MATERIALS SUFFICIENT TO VERIFY CLAIMS BY ALL PARTIES.

Vela Cho responded first:

Absolutely not.

Ira responded second:

Absolutely not is not a filing. I will make it one.

Nema responded after forty seconds:

"Index" is doing violence here.

Oru-Kel did not respond in words. The rail under Tali's hand delivered a pressure shape she had learned only an hour ago.

Refusal history required.

Good.

House Ceyr wanted the map before anyone agreed who could walk through it.

The Ceyr negotiator requested live channel.

Mase said, "No."

Tali said, "Yes, under recorded constraint."

He turned on her.

"Why?"

"Because their offer is already public enough to become a story. If we refuse without exposing the hook, people with sick children will hear that Moonwake rejected medical safety out of pride."

Mase's anger had nowhere honorable to go, so he swallowed it.

"Recorded constraint," he said.

The channel opened with six notices: no custody implied, no manifest access, no warning access, no medical triage bargaining, no auto-expansion, no private sideband. The Ceyr negotiator accepted them all quickly enough to prove she had expected them.

Maerin Ceyr appeared in a narrow frame, middle-aged, silver at one temple, dressed in routeholder black without jewelry except a small pressure seal at her throat. She had the careful warmth of someone trained to comfort families while raising rates.

"Captain Mase. Legal Carrier Eron. House Ceyr recognizes Moonwake's conditional refusal and offers practical shelter."

Tali answered before Mase could.

"Moonwake receives the offer as disputed commercial approach."

Maerin's smile did not change.

"We prefer humanitarian support."

"You may prefer it in your files."

"Then let us be plain. Your ship is approaching crowded authority with damaged systems, contested persons, sealed warning material, and children in need of care. Earth will wrap you in welcome. Mars will wrap you in origin. The Compact will wrap you in emergency. Clean Command will wrap you in necessity. House Ceyr can wrap you in contract."

Mase said, "That is not better."

"It is narrower."

Tali hated that the answer had weight.

Maerin continued, "A contract can be litigated. A rescue can be celebrated before the rescued understand what they lost."

Ira's silent observer marker flashed amber. She wanted that sentence preserved.

Tali preserved it.

"Your escrow clause requests duplicate protected index."

"An index, not content."

Nema's voice entered under observer permission.

"Indexes are content when the subject is personhood."

Maerin inclined her head.

"Advocate Lio. We recognize Glassward sensitivity."

"Do not make sensitivity a smaller word for law."

"Then law. We require enough record structure to verify that Moonwake is not selectively invoking protected status while hiding liabilities that affect berth safety."

Mase laughed once.

"Ceyr wants liability insulation."

"Ceyr wants not to berth a ship whose sealed materials may trigger route conflict, ownership claims, or technical hazards."

"Then do not offer."

Maerin looked at him fully for the first time.

"Captain, if we do not offer, your descendants may dock under people less honest about what they want."

There it was.

Not a threat.

Not exactly.

A market description of danger.

Tali asked, "Why Ceyr?"

Maerin's face stilled.

"Because we were in the beginning."

The briefing table did not move, but Tali felt the ship lean toward the sentence.

Maerin said, "Ceyr underwrote early meridian infrastructure, Kestrel among it. Ceyr profited from delay. Ceyr buried objections under liability partition. Ceyr lost people too, which does not clean the profit. We have spent two centuries learning how much neutrality can cost when it is purchased by not asking what moves through your routes."

Vela's text appeared:

Confession shaped like credential.

Tali agreed.

She also did not dismiss it.

"Does your house claim repair standing?"

"No."

"Custody standing?"

"No."

"Warning standing?"

"No."

"Then what standing do you claim?"

"Operational exposure. If Moonwake remains in contested approach, routeholder lanes will be used, jammed, threatened, or blamed. We can reduce collision and seizure risk."

"In exchange for escrow."

"In exchange for verifiable limits."

Oru-Kel's pressure pulse arrived soft but distinct.

Limit without owner.

Tali wrote it down.

"Would Ceyr accept a no-content risk attestation from the custody table instead of record index?"

Maerin did not answer quickly.

Good.

Real offers had joints.

"Define attestation."

Ira took the channel, voice clipped.

"Ash Archive, Glassward consent office, Orison carried-warning custodian, Moonwake legal carrier, and Compact observer each file bounded statements identifying categories of risk without revealing protected content. Ceyr receives enough operational warning to keep tugs from making stupid choices. Ceyr does not receive index, manifest classes, origin material map, or warning catalog."

"And if a protected record contains a technical hazard directly relevant to berth?"

Vela answered.

"Then you receive the hazard instruction, not the record lineage."

Maerin's smile vanished.

Without it, she looked more tired and less false.

"That might work for berth. It does not work for insurance."

Mase said, "There is the dirt."

Maerin did not deny it.

"Insurance moves ships before virtue finishes drafting. You may despise that and still need it."

Tali thought of the descendant hold. Forty-three green. One hundred ninety-six amber. Seventy-eight red. Twenty-one gray because medical conflict made clean votes cruel.

She thought of the mother from Nursery One asking for gravity therapy.

"House Ceyr may submit a revised offer," Tali said. "No record escrow. No index. No manifest classes. No warning catalog. Medical berth under legal escort. Tug support under operational attestation. Insurance risk accepted by the house or withdrawn."

Maerin said, "That is not commercially realistic."

Mase said, "Neither is returning from a century of being presumed useful."

For a moment Tali saw why people followed him even when he hid things. He could make contempt sound like hull plating.

Maerin looked away, reading something off-channel.

Tali flagged it.

"Sideband?"

"Internal counsel."

"Then the live channel pauses."

"Legal Carrier Eron, we are trying to help."

"Then do not make help indistinguishable from leverage."

She closed the channel.

The table remained silent for three whole seconds, which in emergency law felt like sleep.

Then Valech entered:

"You rejected available safety."

Tali answered, "We rejected record escrow."

"A distinction without oxygen if the ship destabilizes."

"A distinction with law if it survives."

Valech said, "I will note that Moonwake is narrowing options."

Ira said, "Note also that Ceyr can widen them by dropping escrow."

Nema said, "And that index access would compromise consent before consent terms exist."

Oru-Kel's rail pulse came last.

Safety that eats warning becomes warning.

Tali sent that sentence to the descendant channel.

Not as comfort.

As explanation.

Within minutes the public nets began doing what public nets did: simplifying, sharpening, selling. MOONWAKE REJECTS SAFE BERTH. ROUTEHOLDERS DEMAND PROOF. SICK CHILDREN HELD HOSTAGE TO ARCHIVE LAW. HOUSE CEYR OFFERS ACCOUNTABLE SHELTER.

Iven sent Tali one private message:

Is safety always this expensive?

She stared at it for longer than the approach clock could afford.

Then she answered:

Only when someone else owns the door.

House Ceyr resubmitted forty-six minutes later.

The escrow clause remained.

It had a softer name.


Chapter 12: Ira Brings Cinder Jurisdiction

Ira Senn did not wait for House Ceyr's softer name to finish circulating.

She filed.

The Ash Archive docket arrived in layers, because Ira had learned that power often survived by forcing urgency to choose between precision and speed. Her first layer was short enough for public nets to quote badly and legal systems to process quickly:

ASH ARCHIVE ASSERTS LIMITED CINDER JURISDICTION OVER MOONWAKE MATERIALS THAT IDENTIFY, CONTAIN, BENEFIT FROM, OR ENABLE RECONSTRUCTION OF CINDER-ADJACENT ABANDONMENT LOGIC.

The second layer defined every word.

The third named the limits.

The fourth named the violations the Archive would consider seizure, even if the seizing party called them rescue, escrow, harmonization, medical intake, origin verification, or emergency command.

Tali read the fourth layer twice.

Mase read it once and looked older after.

Nema read it while standing very still.

Valech objected before reading finished.

"This is jurisdictional expansion under crisis pressure."

Ira's answer came live, because she had filed and opened channel in the same motion.

"Yes."

The table paused.

Even Lael Shun blinked.

Ira said, "I am not pretending otherwise. The question is whether the expansion is bounded, reviewable, and attached to a known harm, or whether we leave the field open for every other authority to expand in prettier clothing."

Tali marked the sentence for Moonwake law.

Not because she trusted all expansions.

Because honest danger was easier to fence.

Valech said, "The Ash Archive was not created to govern returning generation ships."

"Correct."

"Then it lacks subject authority."

"Over Moonwake as ship, yes. Over Moonwake descendants, yes. Over the warning, yes. Over Earth reception, Mars origin claim, routeholder berth, Glassward memory law, and Compact command, yes. We are not claiming those."

"Your filing says otherwise."

"My filing says that if Moonwake carries proof tied to Cinder abandonment logic, if its launch instruments reused selection categories later visible in Cinder triage, if its records can reopen beneficiary liability, or if any party uses Moonwake proof to launder Cinder conduct into emergency precedent, the Archive has standing."

Vela's observer marker turned green beside Ira.

Not approval.

Custodial alignment.

Mase said, "What does that mean for us?"

Ira looked at him through the channel, and Tali saw the advocate decide not to make kindness the enemy of accuracy.

"It means some of your records cannot be treated as private ship property if they implicate public abandonment crimes."

A muscle jumped in Mase's cheek.

"You are claiming records."

"No. I am claiming objection rights, protected-copy rights under strict category review, and standing to prevent destruction, sale, escrow transfer, or command use."

"That is almost custody."

"Almost is where law lives when the alternative is theft."

Tali did not write that down.

She already knew it would stay.

Nema asked, "Does your filing cover memory-derived materials?"

"If the memory material intersects Cinder categories, yes. If it intersects only Glassward consent law, you lead. If it intersects both, we fight politely until the material remains protected."

Nema almost smiled.

"That is not the worst jurisdictional proposal I have heard today."

"My standards are low and documented," Ira said.

The table accepted the joke badly and gratefully.

Then Lael Shun leaned forward.

"What source binds us to this?"

Ira's face changed.

This was the part she had wanted.

"The Ash Archive order."

Lael said, "Which provision?"

Ira sent the citation.

Not a long one.

That made it worse.

Tali read:

Where abandonment logic is preserved in records, routes, benefits, command instruments, salvage claims, or successor procedures, the Archive may assert protective standing sufficient to prevent conversion of evidence into advantage.

Mase said softly, "Successor procedures."

Nema said, "Command instruments."

Oru-Kel's rail pulse came as a grief hold.

Tali felt the pause enter her bones.

Ira said, "Moonwake was not at Cinder. I am not saying it was. But Moonwake listened through the catastrophe. Moonwake preserved delayed proof. Moonwake's launch design may contain earlier forms of the same selection logic. The Archive does not get to own that. It does get to prevent others from turning that proof into leverage."

"Including us?" Mase asked.

"Especially you, if necessary."

He absorbed it.

Tali saw the same injury cross his face that had crossed the descendant hold when Sevi named the hidden casualty ledger. Not betrayal by an enemy. Something harder: the discovery that protection could become a way of keeping accounts closed.

"What records do you seek?" Tali asked.

Ira answered with prepared exactness.

"First, hash verification of all Moonwake records tagged with abandonment, triage, dependency, survival threshold, return deferral, mission benefit, or population continuity."

"No content."

"Correct. Hash only."

"Second?"

"Protected category map generated by Moonwake legal staff, not by external search. You tell us where the dangerous rooms might be. We do not enter."

Vela's text appeared:

Unless smoke emerges.

Ira said, "Unless smoke emerges."

Mase said, "Third?"

"A preservation order over the casualty ledger."

There.

The room changed.

Tali did not look at Mase.

That was courtesy.

Not mercy.

Mase said, "The ledger is internal ship command."

Ira said, "The ledger may be internal ship command. It may also document how Moonwake applied abandonment logic after receiving external war evidence, route silence, or warning material. Until reviewed under ship law and protected table constraint, no one destroys, edits, seals, or trades it."

"No one was going to destroy it."

Tali heard the wrong tense.

So did everyone else.

Mase heard it after, which was worse.

He placed both hands flat on the table.

"I have not destroyed the ledger."

Ira said, "Then my order preserves what exists."

Valech intervened.

"This is becoming a tribunal before docking."

Ira turned on him.

"No. This is preventing docking from becoming amnesty."

The sentence hit the table with enough force to make even the approach clock seem quiet.

Earth filed a concern that excessive legal complexity endangered humanitarian reception.

Mars filed a conditional support note that Cinder-adjacent categories might overlap pre-gate origin instruments.

House Ceyr filed an objection that Ash Archive standing could impair neutral berth insurance.

Vela denied the objection as premature and self-revealing.

Tali had never loved an administrative phrase before.

The descendant channel began to fill with questions.

Are they taking our records?

Can Ash law override ship law?

Is the casualty ledger about us?

Did command hide deaths?

Do we still get hospitals?

Iven sent no message.

That silence bothered Tali more than panic.

She opened a public ship statement and wrote slowly:

Ash Archive filing does not transfer Moonwake custody. It preserves disputed categories from destruction, escrow, command use, or outside ownership. Moonwake ship law remains active. Descendant rights remain active. Medical contact remains required under conditional terms. The casualty ledger is now under preservation and will be reviewed under ship law with protected observers.

Mase read it before she sent.

"Add that I consent."

Tali looked at him.

"Do you?"

His face closed, then opened by force.

"No. Add that I submit."

She did.

That was better.

Not cleaner.

Better.

The statement went public.

For nine seconds, nothing answered.

Then Sevi sent one line from the elder channel:

Submission is not repair, but it is a door.

Tali forwarded it to Mase.

He read it.

He did not thank her.

Ira's filing entered the public record at thirteen hours eight minutes to approach decision.

The table had gained another boundary.

It had also gained another wound.

Moonwake held both because there was nowhere else to put them.


Chapter 13: The Warning Under Seal

The warning had names before it had permission.

Earth called it hazard material.

Mars called it origin-adjacent convergence data.

Clean Command called it operational timing.

House Ceyr called it insurable risk structure.

The public nets called it the moonfire file by noon, because fear liked poetry when poetry did not have to be accurate.

Moonwake called it sealed.

Tali wrote the release protocol in the legal gallery with Mase on one side, Nema on the other, and Oru-Kel's pressure channel warming the rail beneath her left hand. Ira watched from the Ash Archive table. Vela watched Ira watching. Lael Shun had moved from observer status to constrained participant after filing seven reservations and receiving seven acknowledgments that did not flatter the Compact.

Valech was present because excluding him would have made him more dangerous.

That was not law.

It was weather.

The approach clock showed twelve hours thirty-one minutes.

Moonwake's medical board had added three amber respiratory cases and one red vestibular case. The mother from Nursery One had not sent Tali another message, which felt like accusation even though Tali knew better than to turn silence into evidence.

Tali opened the draft.

"Moonwake proposes public release of warning index categories without full pattern disclosure."

Valech said, "Define index."

Nema said, "Carefully."

Tali did not smile.

"Index means bounded descriptive labels sufficient to identify the existence, broad subject, custody holder, access restrictions, and operational relevance of sealed warning components. Index does not include pulse sequence, timing intervals, refusal history details, model-ready variables, source-carrier reconstruction, or living custodian chain beyond named offices."

Vela sent:

Better than Ceyr's index.

Ira said aloud, "The Archive can work with that."

Valech leaned forward.

"Can emergency planners work with it?"

"Enough to know what they do not have," Tali said.

"That is not planning."

Oru-Kel's rail pulse arrived before Ren translated:

Knowing absence prevents false fullness.

Valech looked at the rail as if it had insulted him personally.

Perhaps it had.

Mase asked, "What categories?"

Tali projected the list.

One: Convergence timing uncertainty.

Two: Refusal pause markers.

Three: Mass movement hazard.

Four: Route-selection exclusion risk.

Five: Memory-derived interpretation hazard.

Six: Cinder-adjacent abandonment trigger.

Seven: Living custodian dependency.

Eight: Non-human carried-warning context.

Nine: Machine listener restriction.

Ten: Sealed full-pattern sequence.

No one spoke.

That was how Tali knew the list had done its job.

It did not satisfy anyone.

It frightened everyone in the correct direction.

Lael Shun read the list twice.

"Category six will ignite public argument."

Ira said, "Public argument is already burning. This keeps it from pretending there is no fuel."

"Category five puts Glassward in the center."

Nema said, "Glassward is already in the center. I object only to being named without consent logs attached."

"Those belong to the next custody table," Tali said.

Nema nodded once and, mercifully, did not ask for more while the index was still bleeding objections.

Mase did.

With his eyes.

Later, Tali thought at him, which was unfair because he could not hear thoughts and fair because he had a ledger under preservation.

Valech tapped category two.

"Refusal pause markers cannot remain sealed if they affect operational timing."

Oru-Kel's pressure came as a low refusal.

Ren, joining late and breathless, translated while still fastening his channel harness.

"They can remain sealed until an operation approaches a pause trigger. Then the trigger is invoked without revealing the full carried history."

"Who decides proximity?"

"The custodian chain."

"Which includes Moonwake now."

Tali said, "Under co-custody, not ownership."

"A ship refusing docking now holds timing veto over route operations."

Ira said, "A command coalition trying to route operations through sealed alien-warning material does not get to complain that custody is inconvenient."

Valech ignored the tone and attacked the structure.

"If the full pattern remains sealed, any model we build is blind."

Nema said, "No. It is constrained."

"Constrained models fail."

"Unconstrained models lie."

Tali marked that too.

The warning index had begun as a compromise. In practice it became a mirror. Every authority saw the thing it most wanted to take.

Earth wanted medical exception language attached to category ten.

Mars wanted origin instruments cross-referenced under category five.

House Ceyr wanted operational relevance expanded into escrow relevance.

Clean Command wanted category one quantified.

The public wanted to know whether the moonfire file meant evacuation, rationing, or war.

Moonwake wanted gravity therapy without surrender.

Tali wanted sleep so badly that for a moment she understood every bad law ever written by tired people.

She drank nutrient water instead.

It tasted like a compromise between algae and apology.

The table began line review.

Category one received a public range: convergence risk within the current approach window, precision sealed.

Category two received a public description: warning contains protected pauses where action must stop before converting loss into acceptable margin.

Valech objected to the phrase acceptable margin.

Ira said, "Good."

Category three stated that simultaneous mass movement through crowded meridian lanes could amplify rather than reduce hazard.

House Ceyr filed support so quickly that Vela wrote:

Even useful agreement can smell bad.

Category four named route-selection exclusion risk without naming which marginal habitats might be selected out by a clean model.

That omission hurt Tali.

Oru-Kel pulsed grief hold, and she let the hurt remain instead of editing around it.

Category five was the hardest.

Nema insisted:

NO MEMORY-DERIVED MATERIAL MAY ENTER WARNING MODELING WITHOUT CONSENT HISTORY, BENEFIT TRACE, AND PERSONHOOD REVIEW.

Valech said, "That language blocks emergency cognition tools."

Nema said, "It blocks theft wearing a clock."

"If a tool saves lives?"

"Then it can survive a consent log."

Tali watched Mase listen to that with the expression of a man hearing his future censure assemble from other people's principles.

Category six linked Cinder-adjacent abandonment triggers to Ash Archive standing.

Earth objected.

Mars did not.

That was its own revelation.

Category seven named living custodian dependency: Orison carried-warning custodian, Moonwake legal carrier, Ash Archive protective standing where Cinder categories apply, Glassward consent advocate where memory categories apply, Compact observer under constrained participation.

Lael Shun said, "This is not elegant."

Tali answered, "No."

"That was not criticism."

"I know."

Category eight preserved non-human context.

Category nine permitted machine listening only as named listener, not owner, with output constrained by pause authority.

Category ten remained sealed.

The full pattern.

The thing everyone wanted because it promised the relief of not arguing anymore.

Tali stared at the line for a long time.

Then she added:

Full-pattern sequence remains sealed until access conditions are met by plural custodian chain or until a bounded trigger requires limited invocation. No single authority may compel full release by declaring emergency.

Valech said, "Then we may lose the window."

Oru-Kel answered without translation delay:

You may also lose yourselves.

No one liked that.

No one could file it away as irrelevant.

Mase authorized the index release under ship law. Ira attached Ash preservation notice. Nema attached Glassward consent reservation. Oru-Kel attached carried-warning refusal history marker without content. Lael attached Compact participation objection and acknowledgment. Valech attached operational insufficiency notice.

Tali attached the descendant hold result.

That felt important.

Not because it made the warning safer.

Because the people who would live under the consequences had said no home yet before the powerful could call secrecy protection and disclosure duty.

At twelve hours six minutes, Moonwake released the warning index.

The nets went wild.

Earth requested emergency family liaison.

Mars requested technical review.

Ceyr requested revised berth terms.

Valech requested immediate modeling session.

Nema requested consent logs.

Ira requested a protected copy of all objections.

Oru-Kel requested silence.

That last request was the only one Tali granted immediately.

For sixty seconds, Moonwake muted all external channels except medical emergency bands and hull safety traffic.

The ship listened to itself.

Air through vents.

Feet in corridors.

Children asking adults what sealed meant.

Old metal carrying new fear.

At the end of the minute, Tali reopened the table.

The warning remained under seal.

But now everyone could see the shape of the locked door.


Chapter 14: Glassward Wants Consent Logs

Nema Lio did not ask for trust.

That was the first reason Tali began to.

Glassward's formal demand arrived nine minutes after the warning index release, attached to no threat, no berth offer, no medical exception, and no claim of ancestral priority. It was a narrow file with an ugly title:

CONSENT HISTORY REQUIREMENT FOR MEMORY-DERIVED WARNING INTERPRETATION.

The ugliness reassured Tali. Beautiful titles had been doing too much work today.

Nema opened channel from a small room with white walls and no visible windows. The Glassward consent placeholder behind her was not a flag or seal. It was a list of names blurred beyond reading.

That, too, reassured Tali.

"Moonwake," Nema said. "Glassward requests consent logs before any memory-derived material enters emergency modeling."

Valech said, "We have no time for another archive."

Nema turned toward his frame.

"Then do not use memory-derived material."

"The warning index names memory-derived interpretation hazard. If that hazard affects the model, the model must address it."

"Correct."

"Then we need the material."

"No. You need to know whether the material can lawfully speak."

Tali felt the sentence strike somewhere near the place Oru-Kel's rail pulses had been teaching her to listen.

Whether material can lawfully speak.

Moonwake had treated records as patient things. Old things. Dangerous things. Useful things. It had not always asked whether a record had been made from someone who had been given the chance to refuse becoming one.

Mase said, "What counts as memory-derived here?"

Nema sent a list.

One: cognitive traces embedded in launch-era education systems.

Two: tutor ecology patterns trained from dependent crew, students, patients, or colonist candidates.

Three: Glassward continuity fragments or forked personhood records.

Four: interpretive heuristics derived from nonconsensual testing of memory response under isolation.

Five: any Moonwake learning song, nursery protocol, legal cadence, or warning translation that carries unidentified source-person structure.

Tali read item five three times.

Her mother's air-cycle rhyme waited inside her like a room with the light off.

"You are saying our childhood may require consent review before it helps save people."

Nema did not flinch.

"I am saying your childhood may already be helping people without knowing what it cost someone else."

Mase's voice hardened.

"Careful."

Nema looked at him.

"That was careful."

Tali almost laughed, because it was her own sentence from days and centuries ago.

She did not laugh.

Nothing in the room was funny.

Ira asked, "What do you need immediately?"

"Not full content," Nema said. "Logs."

Valech rubbed his forehead.

"Logs are content."

"Sometimes. These can be category confirmations."

Nema projected a consent log schema.

Source category.

Acquisition condition.

Consent status.

Dependency status.

Withdrawal possibility.

Benefit trace.

Current use.

Personhood risk.

Emergency admissibility recommendation.

Tali saw the table stiffen at withdrawal possibility.

There were concepts powerful systems hated because they implied the useful thing might leave.

Oru-Kel's rail pulse came warm.

Refusal after giving is still refusal.

Nema nodded as if she had heard it in her own language.

"Yes."

Valech said, "If a memory-derived heuristic identifies a route that prevents mass casualties, and consent status is unknown, you would block it?"

"I would label it inadmissible until a bounded emergency review determines whether use creates personhood harm, repeats coercion, or converts stolen cognition into command authority."

"That is blocking."

"That is stopping your sentence before it turns unknown people into tools."

"People may die during your review."

"People already died during other people's efficiencies."

The table went silent.

Tali thought of the index category: memory-derived interpretation hazard. It had seemed abstract when she wrote it. Now it had her mother's voice.

She opened Moonwake's internal archive query.

Learning song: air-cycle rhyme.

The system returned seven records.

Four were ordinary: Shipboard Education Board, generation three, water conservation cadence; revised generation five; meter adapted for low-oxygen drills; nursery-approved.

Three were sealed under launch inheritance.

Tali's skin went cold.

Mase saw her face.

"What?"

She sent the query result to the protected table.

Nema closed her eyes.

Not triumph.

Grief.

"Do not open the sealed records yet," she said.

Mase said, "They are nursery materials."

"They are sealed nursery materials."

Ira whispered, "That distinction keeps appearing."

Tali could not stop looking at the three sealed lines.

Launch inheritance. Launch inheritance. Launch inheritance.

Her mother had sung the rhyme while repairing filters. Her father had tapped it against ration tins. Children used it to time breath games. Tali had hummed it when afraid of pressure doors. If it contained someone else's coerced memory structure, then the theft had not been hidden in a vault.

It had been loved.

That was worse.

Not because love made theft larger.

Because love made repair harder.

Nema's voice softened.

"Legal Carrier Eron, Glassward does not claim your song."

Tali looked up.

"I know."

"I need that said before anyone turns this into outside seizure."

"Say it formally."

Nema did.

Glassward does not claim Moonwake cultural materials. Glassward requests consent logs and personhood review before memory-derived structures are used in emergency modeling or external command.

Tali attached Moonwake response:

Moonwake preserves cultural custody while submitting sealed source categories to consent review. No nursery material may be extracted, modeled, traded, or used for route command until source status is logged.

Mase said, "That may slow the model."

"Yes."

"Children need hospitals."

"Yes."

He looked angry enough to break something and disciplined enough not to.

"I hate this."

Tali said, "So do I."

Nema said, "Good. If you enjoyed consent law, you would probably be doing it wrong."

That landed better than the joke deserved because everyone was starving for a humane sentence.

Valech recovered first.

"I will accept category logs, but not veto over emergency modeling."

"You do not have authority to accept or refuse Glassward law," Nema said.

"Emergency authority can override."

Lael Shun entered quietly.

"Not yet it cannot."

Everyone turned.

Lael had said very little since the warning index. Now her Compact frame looked smaller, not weaker.

"The Compact is not whole enough to override every standing jurisdiction in the same hour it asks them to cooperate."

Valech's expression hardened.

"Then convene authority."

Lael said, "I will."

Tali felt the next chapter of the crisis arrive before anyone named it.

Broken table.

No one whole enough to command.

Everyone implicated enough to matter.

Nema sent the first consent log request to Moonwake under protected seal.

Tali accepted it.

The system asked for carrier confirmation.

She placed her thumb against the slate.

For a moment she smelled warm filters, old metal, her mother's hands, nutrient broth, and fear.

Then the slate chimed.

CONSENT REVIEW OPENED.

The sealed nursery records did not open.

But they stopped pretending to be harmless.


Chapter 15: Lael Shun Convenes the Broken Table

Lael Shun convened authority by admitting she did not have it.

That was not how Compact officers were trained to begin.

Tali had read enough reception law, emergency charters, and launch-era command procedures to know the old shape of power. Authority entered first as certainty. It named the room. It defined the terms. It established who could speak, who could witness, who could object later if they survived the decision. It made itself sound inevitable before anyone had the chance to call it partial.

Lael did the opposite.

Her summons arrived on every active channel with no seal music, no heroic banner, no unified emergency crest:

COMPACT OBSERVER LAEL SHUN REQUESTS BROKEN TABLE SESSION.

REQUEST, NOT ORDER.

PURPOSE: IDENTIFY WHO CAN LAWFULLY PARTICIPATE IN TIME-CRITICAL MODELING WITHOUT PRETENDING ANY SINGLE AUTHORITY IS WHOLE.

Valech objected in thirty-two seconds.

Earth asked whether request language weakened reception legitimacy.

Mars asked whether origin claim status would be preserved.

House Ceyr asked whether routeholder operational exposure granted voting participation.

Glassward asked for consent-rights protection before agenda adoption.

The Ash Archive asked whether evidence objections would bind absent parties.

Orison sent a pressure pulse Tali had begun to recognize as guarded approval.

Moonwake's descendant channel asked whether broken meant dangerous.

Tali answered that one herself:

All tables are dangerous. Broken tables are at least honest about the crack.

Iven sent back:

That sounds like something adults say before making children wait.

Tali stared at the line.

Then she answered:

Yes.

It was the only honest reply she had.

The session opened in the Ash Archive room because no one trusted the Compact chamber, Earth reception suite, Mars origin office, Ceyr negotiation hall, Glassward consent forum, Orison carried-warning channel, or Moonwake shipboard assembly to feel neutral. The Archive was not neutral either. It had just learned to put its partiality on the table before anyone leaned on it.

Lael appeared seated, not standing. That mattered. Her frame showed hands, table, water cup, recording light, and the plain gray wall behind her. No flags. No map. No old Compact starburst.

"This is not a Compact command session," she said.

Valech's face tightened.

"Then it cannot issue operational decisions."

"Correct."

That answer unsettled him more than contradiction would have.

Lael continued, "This is a broken table session to identify bounded authority, objection rights, evidence protections, and minimum participation required before any emergency model can be treated as lawful input."

Earth's representative, Commissioner Halev, spoke with the soft voice of institutional welcome.

"Earth cannot accept a process that delays humanitarian reception."

Mase said, "Earth can accept many things. It has been practicing on Moonwake since first hail."

Tali did not look at him, but she felt the descendant channel brighten with vicious gratitude.

Lael said, "Humanitarian reception is on agenda."

Halev said, "First?"

"No."

"That is unacceptable."

"So is pretending medical need transfers custody."

Nema's eyes flicked toward Lael with surprised respect.

Tali recorded the exchange.

Mars came next through Djen Rook, whose voice still carried the dry precision of people who did not want to be thanked for telling partial truth.

"Mars requires origin mission materials preserved from unilateral reinterpretation."

Ira said, "Mars requires many things. State which are lawful."

Djen accepted the correction with a nod.

"Mars claims standing over launch instruments designed, funded, or administered through Martian predecessor offices. Mars does not claim Moonwake persons. Mars does not claim warning ownership. Mars does claim right to inspect origin categories before Earth, Ceyr, or Compact emergency offices convert them into rescue narrative."

Vela wrote:

Less bad than expected.

Ira said, "That is not a legal category."

Vela answered aloud, "No, but it is often the most we get before lunch."

The table did not laugh.

It breathed.

Lael let the breath exist, then called House Ceyr.

Maerin Ceyr had removed the silver pressure seal from her throat. Or perhaps the camera angle no longer showed it. Tali suspected the former and distrusted herself for noticing.

"House Ceyr claims operational exposure," Maerin said. "We do not claim custody, personhood authority, warning ownership, or evidence control. We request participation in modeling only where route lanes, tug operations, berth safety, and insurance triggers are affected."

Vela said, "Insurance triggers do not get voting status."

"They move ships."

"So do bribes."

Maerin's face cooled.

"House Ceyr does not request trust. We request bounded participation where our infrastructure will be used or blamed."

Tali said, "No record escrow."

"Ceyr reserves position."

"Moonwake reserves refusal."

Lael marked both.

Then came Glassward.

Nema did not perform dignity. She looked tired, angry, and exact.

"Glassward claims consent standing over memory-derived materials, continuity fragments, personhood-risk traces, and any cognition tool trained on source persons without clear permission. We do not claim Moonwake culture. We do not claim origin mission command. We do not claim warning ownership. We require consent logs before protected materials enter models."

Valech said, "And if the model fails without them?"

"Then the model was never lawful enough to trust."

He turned to Lael.

"Are you going to let every participant bring a veto?"

Lael said, "No."

For one hopeful second, Valech looked relieved.

Lael continued, "I am going to ask every participant to name what they can lawfully stop."

The relief disappeared.

Oru-Kel's channel spoke through Ren Okoye and Third-Voice together.

"Orison claims carried-warning context, refusal pause markers, living custodian chain, non-human relation integrity, and machine-listener limits. We do not claim human command. We do not claim Moonwake. We do not claim fear. We stop extraction."

Lael marked:

ORISON CAN STOP EXTRACTION.

Tali felt the words settle into the table like bolts.

Ira followed.

"Ash Archive claims protective standing over Cinder-adjacent abandonment logic, successor procedures, evidence conversion into advantage, protected copies, preservation orders, and public docket where secrecy becomes leverage. We do not claim ship custody. We do not claim survivor simplicity. We stop amnesty by docking."

Lael marked:

ASH ARCHIVE CAN STOP AMNESTY BY DOCKING.

Mase looked down.

Tali wished he would not.

Then it was Moonwake's turn.

Lael said, "Legal Carrier Eron."

Tali had known the call was coming. Knowing did not make her lungs more cooperative.

She opened Moonwake's statement and did not read it yet.

Instead she looked at the descendant channel: green, amber, red, gray, names, kin clusters, children listening where parents allowed. The ship was not a legal theory. It was a crowd of people inside metal deciding how not to become cargo.

"Moonwake claims ship law over its living descendants, internal assemblies, cultural materials, untransferred records, casualty ledger review, medical escort terms, and the right to refuse homecoming language until custody terms exist. Moonwake does not claim warning ownership. Moonwake does not claim immunity from evidence duties. Moonwake does not claim the right to hide origin theft behind love."

The last sentence cost her more than she expected.

Mase looked at her then.

Nema did too.

Lael marked:

MOONWAKE CAN STOP CUSTODY DISGUISED AS WELCOME.

Tali had to swallow before she could continue.

"Moonwake can also stop captain-only waiver."

Mase said, "Confirmed."

That was a small thing and a large one.

Lael marked it.

Valech had waited longer than Tali expected.

When Lael called him, he did not pretend softness.

"Emergency planning claims authority to model convergence risk, assign time-critical route actions, coordinate medical reception, prevent collision, and recommend command structure before the approach window closes. We do not claim record ownership. We do not claim permanent sovereignty. We do claim that a table which can only stop cannot save."

There.

The cleanest version of his argument.

Tali hated it less when he said it without decoration.

Lael said, "What can you lawfully stop?"

Valech paused.

"Operational paralysis."

Ira said, "Define without insulting everyone."

Valech's jaw set.

"A condition in which no authorized action can occur before preventable loss because every participant holds unbounded objection rights."

Lael marked:

EMERGENCY PLANNING CAN STOP UNBOUNDED PARALYSIS, IF BOUNDARIES ARE DEFINED.

Valech stared at the qualifier.

"If boundaries are defined."

"Yes."

"By whom?"

Lael looked at the record they had made.

"Apparently by all the people whose absence would make the model unlawful."

That was the broken table.

Not consensus.

Not unity.

A map of who could stop which theft before survival used it.

The next argument lasted forty-one minutes.

They fought over quorum. Earth wanted medical priority to count as automatic participation. Mars wanted origin categories to require dual confirmation. Ceyr wanted infrastructure exposure to trigger operational vote. Glassward wanted consent issues removed from majority rule. Orison refused majority language entirely. The Ash Archive rejected any quorum that excluded harmed categories because they were inconvenient to count. Moonwake refused any structure where descendants became subject matter without witness rights.

Valech called it impossible.

Lael called it draft one.

At eleven hours eight minutes, the broken table produced its first usable rule:

No model output may be presented as emergency authority unless it lists which participating jurisdictions can lawfully object, which protected categories were excluded, which pauses remain active, and which populations bear unmodeled risk.

It was not enough.

It was the first sentence today that made false simplicity harder.

Tali sent it to the descendant channel.

Iven replied:

Does this mean adults have to show their work?

Tali almost smiled.

Yes.

He sent back:

Finally.

The second usable rule followed:

Medical contact may proceed under escort without converting Moonwake persons into passengers, patients into custody subjects, or treatment records into manifest access.

The mother from Nursery One sent no words.

Only a green acknowledgment.

Tali stared at it until the blur in her eyes cleared.

Lael closed the session with no triumph.

"The table remains broken," she said. "It is therefore still visible. Emergency modeling may begin under these constraints."

Valech said, "The first model will fail."

No one contradicted him.

That, too, was honest.

Outside the windowless channels, Moonwake continued toward the crowded lanes, not safe, not received, not alone.

For the first time since arrival, Tali understood that broken did not always mean useless.

Sometimes broken meant no one could hide the join.


Chapter 16: The First Model Fails Cleanly

The first model saved almost everyone.

That was the problem.

Valech did not present it as triumph. To his credit, or to his discipline, he looked grim when the output stabilized over the broken table. Twelve hours of argument had not made him kinder, but it had taught him which words would be treated as weapons if he used them too quickly.

"Model one," he said. "Unified routing priority with medical corridor preservation, Moonwake controlled contact, partial warning-index incorporation, no full-pattern release, no memory-derived materials pending consent review, Orison pause markers treated as advisory constraints."

Oru-Kel's rail pulse tightened.

Advisory is often disobedience in formal clothes.

Ren translated that exactly.

Valech did not react.

"Simulated under current traffic, propulsion, and lane congestion data. Assumptions listed. Exclusions listed. Unmodeled categories listed."

Lael said, "Read the exclusions aloud before outcome."

Valech's eyes moved once toward her.

"That is inefficient."

"That is the rule."

He read.

"Excluded: full carried-warning sequence; sealed nursery source records; consent-unclear memory heuristics; Cinder-adjacent protected evidence content; casualty ledger data; nonpublic descendant medical details; routeholder escrow index; unidentified marginal habitat dependency claims; two Orison relation markers awaiting custodian translation."

Ira said, "Unidentified marginal habitat dependency claims is carrying too much."

Valech said, "It is the category label."

"Then unpack it."

He looked at the model, then at the clock.

Ten hours forty-nine minutes.

"Three small habitats and one mobile repair flotilla assert dependency on lanes that the model reallocates away from non-priority traffic. Their claims lack full verification."

Vela said, "Names."

"Mallow Ring. Three-Seam Cooperative. Anvil Child Cluster. The repair flotilla Saint Errant."

The names entered the room like people.

Tali had not known them.

That did not make them smaller.

Mase said, "Population?"

Valech hesitated.

"Verified total: forty-one thousand six hundred. Claimed dependent additional: unknown."

Earth's Halev said, "Compared to primary lane population exposure?"

"Nine point two million."

There it was.

The first clean knife.

Tali felt the table lean toward the old arithmetic because arithmetic was such a relief when responsibility hurt.

Nine point two million against forty-one thousand six hundred plus unknown.

Almost everyone.

The model displayed survival bands in calm color. Green for primary worlds. Blue for Moonwake contact probability. Amber for contested lanes. Red for marginal habitats whose lane access fell below sustainment threshold during the convergence window.

There were only four red marks.

Only.

Tali hated the word before anyone said it.

No one did, which meant everyone was thinking it.

Valech continued.

"Model one prevents cascade collision in primary lanes, preserves Earth-Mars medical corridor, allows Moonwake controlled contact within six hours, and maintains sufficient routeholder tug availability to prevent drift lock. It fails to preserve low-priority independent lane access for the named marginal habitats if convergence arrives on the early side of uncertainty."

Lael asked, "Does the model show that as failure?"

"It shows it as acceptable loss within emergency threshold."

The words did not echo.

They did not need to.

Ira closed her eyes.

Oru-Kel's channel went very still.

Nema said, "No."

Valech's face hardened.

"No is not analysis."

"No is sometimes the first analysis."

He turned to Tali.

"Moonwake?"

She should have had a sentence ready.

Legal carriers were supposed to have sentences ready. It was practically the job.

Instead she looked at the red marks.

Mallow Ring. Three-Seam Cooperative. Anvil Child Cluster. Saint Errant.

Names she had not known ten seconds ago, now attached to whether Moonwake would get hospitals, whether Nursery One would breathe easier, whether the warning could be modeled fast enough to save primary lanes, whether the broken table would become an anecdote told by survivors who preferred not to count the absent.

Iven's earlier message came back:

Does this mean adults have to show their work?

Yes.

Tali said, "Show the work."

Valech's mouth tightened.
